$/P11N_ICH
!_MIzTEN
!_HEUEED
{=CB13 divides as ICH MIzT EN HEUEED, presumably "While I might on}
{head". But I cannot make sense of this and I assume there is textual}
{corruption. This passage in Harley 2253´s version is entirely about}
{Maximian´s wife and the pronoun is consistently HUE. Here there is}
{clearly a somewhat different text because Maximian´s friends are}
{introduced. I suspect that HI or HY in the exemplar for "they" has}
{been taken to be for the similarly spelled first person singular which}
{the Digby scribe here translates to his preferred spelling ICH. This}
{would explain the form MIzTEN which would then be correctly plural.}
{HEUEED may be a rationalisation to the word for "head" from exemplar}
{HEED or HEEDE. The whole might have been: "they consider me to be a bad}
{lot, while they might take heed"=}
{\}
$beat/vSpp_*I-BET+EN $ge-/xp-vpp_*I- $/vSpp_+EN
$havenot/vpt11_N+E+DDE $n-/xp-neg_N+ $/vpt13[V]_+DDE
$/P11N+H_ICH
!_HOE
{=This is not plausible as a form for the accusative of the third person}
{singular of the feminine personal pronoun nor of the plural. We therefore}
{have two subjects and the textual corruption seems to continue. I}
{suggest the original would have had "had they [or she] not beaten me"}
{with ICH being erroneously substituted for ME. Or, syntactically more}
{probable, "if I had not been beaten", with HOE being written for BE}
{(via BOE?) which fits the rhyme-scheme and would be a form of the past}
{participle of the verb "be"=}
{\}

$westlooker/n{rh}_WEST-LOK+ERE $looker/n-k{rh}_-LOK+ERE $-er/xs-agt-n{rh}_+ERE
{=This is better than "stye-looker" - "the bleary-eyed one" as suggested}
{by Ross (1935) and it is backed up by Medulla Grammatice entries for}
{west-lokere i.e. ´a lokere west´ etc glossing ´petus´ (Classical Latin}
{´paetus´. The meaning seems to be "divergent squinter", "away-looker" i.e.}
{"one that has its eyes on the side of the head". Cf. SID-LOKERE,}
{BRODLOKERE and WALDENEIE. See further Laing (1998) - Medium Aevum=}

$roughlooker?/n_ROU+LOK+ERE $looker?/n-k_+LOK+ERE $-er/xs-agt-n_+ERE
{=This is taking what has been read LEKERE to read LOKERE which I believe}
{it plausibly does. "Rough-looker" I take to mean "fierce-looker" or}
{"glarer" - see Laing (1998) - Medium Aevum. Ross (1935) reads +LEKERE}
{and suggests "frisker" from OScand leika OE la:can, this is not}
{otherwise recorded before C19. However the verb leiken is fine at this}
{date and the formation seems possible though unusual in not having -EIK-}
{or -AIK-. Cf Digby 86 Debate of Body and Soul LEIKEN vi. MED, for some}
{reason, seems to accept "frisker" for the form here, but to think}
{LEKERE below is a form of the word for "lecher". I assume that one}
{ought to take the two LEKEREs as being the same word not different}
{ones if one reads LEKERE for both. The rhyme here, however, supports}
{the reading LOKERE rather than LEKERE though cf. LEKERE below. Ross}
{may be right but ROU is also difficult as a form from OE rae:w. Digby}
{does however have ROWE in rhyme in the Thrush and the Nightingale. If}
{LOKERE is accepted however, "rough", meaning "fierce" from OE ru:h, ru:g,}
{ru:we is the best reading - see MED s.v. rough(e adj and OED s.v.row}
{a1 and row adv}. Raw-looker is perhaps equally possible, from OE}
{hre:aw/hre:ow. See MED raue aj. NB. "raw-eyes" implies prominent eyes}
{and OED row a2, cf. raw-eyed. See further also Laing (1998)=}
{\}

$cower-on-hill/n{rh}_COURE-HO+HULLE $on{p}/pr_-HO+ $hill/n-pr-k{rh}_+HULLE
{=See Laing (1998). MS appears to have TOURE HOHULLE. Ross (1935) emends to}
{TOURE-TOHULLE and translates "the one who directs one´s steps to the}
{hills". I don´t think this plausible or a justifiable emendation.}
{Digby has at least three examples of HON for "on", while HO for "on"}
{is found once each in Clerico et Puella and in the Egerton 613 Somer}
{is comen verse. What you do on a hill if you´re a hare is not "tour"}
{"tower" or "turn" but "cower" in the sense "lie low, skulk". Ross}
{points out that hares can outrun dogs uphill because of their leg}
{shape, but once on the hill they must skulk because down-hill they}
{are at a considerable disadvantage and once at the summit they will}
{be caught. See G.E. Evans and D. Thomson, The Leaping Hare, London}
{1972, p. 33. This phrase does not appear in any form in MED or OED,}
{apparently. For examples which can be added to of T-shape for C in}
{Digby see CON 119ra l 14, COME 119vb l 28, COMEy 120ra l 10, CUNNE}
{120rb line 19, COME 120va line 22 etc. and cf. COUARD for "toward"}
{fol. 121vb line 33=}
{\}

{=No space break between this text and the previous and no coloured}
{initial introducing it. Whether or not the texts were separate in his}
{exemplar, and in spite of the intervening ´amen´, the scribe here treats}
{the two as a single text, possibly also as a unit with ´The Debate}
{between the Body and the Soul´ fols 195v-197v, not included in this}
{sample because it is in mixed language, but tagged separately as text}
{no. 222. As with the previous two texts, the four lines of each}
{rhyming quatrain are joined by wiggly braces in the right margin=}
{~f198r}
